  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_65d575fd-63cd-4f04-9de1-2f0d9bbc6d11'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1757060-i-often-think-to-read-this-as-it-fits-the">I often think to read this as it fits the style of books I enjoy, from what I hear about it. However, what holds me back is that I&#39;ve heard there is death of a main character. I fear that would break my heart. (I had a hard time getting over the ending of Romeo and Juliette.) Is it deeply heartbreaking? Or is there a redeeming quality to leave you at peace in the end?</a>
</div>

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">It is sad, yes, but it is not sudden. It kind of comes slowly, and you can tell when it might happen. It broke my heart, but I really enjoyed the book<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">It is sad, yes, but it is not sudden. It kind of comes slowly, and you can tell when it might happen. It broke my heart, but I really enjoyed the book and you will too!<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1495136-why-is-this-edition-of-little-women-only">Why is this edition of Little Women only 346 pages? This is the edition I bought....am I missing something?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1495136'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F15796908-little-women">like</a></span>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/15962370-sarah"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1357027577i/15962370._UX100_CR0,12,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/15962370-sarah">Sarah</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Most editions published in the US contain both Parts 1 and 2 of "Little Women", a total of 47 chapters. In Britain and Australia (and presumably other<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Most editions published in the US contain both Parts 1 and 2 of "Little Women", a total of 47 chapters. In Britain and Australia (and presumably other countries, too), the two parts seem to be published separately, as "Little Women" (Chapters 1-24) and "Good Wives" (Chapters 25-47) respectively.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_3206142305" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3206142305" />
    <a title=" Teodora " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69603149-teodora"><img alt=" Teodora " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582144281p2/69603149.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3206142305?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title=" Teodora " class="user" itemprop="url" name=" Teodora " href="/user/show/69603149-teodora"> Teodora </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847495877" href="/book/show/30733607-little-women">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69603149-teodora?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3206142305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16760852978761770909">3.45/5 ⭐<br/><br/><b>Full review on my Blog:</b> <a href='https://thedacianshewolf.home.blog/2020/03/23/little-women-by-louisa-may-alcott/' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Dacian She-Wolf</a> 🐺<br/><br/>So, feeling classical and pinky, I’ve decided to read <i>
  <b>Little Women</b>
</i>.<br/>Finally.<br/><br/>I felt kind of intellectual when I carried it around with me in trains and subways, not gonna lie. But leaving that aside, I quite enjoyed it. I could actually label it as a cute and educative reading. But that's it.<br/><br/>There’s no much to say about it though. Everyone knows what’s it about. And if you don’t, you can find out what happens from <i>
  <b>Friends</b>
</i>. Rachel and Joe</span>
  <span id="freeText16760852978761770909" style="display:none">3.45/5 ⭐<br><br><b>Full review on my Blog:</b> <a href="https://thedacianshewolf.home.blog/2020/03/23/little-women-by-louisa-may-alcott/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dacian She-Wolf</a> 🐺<br><br>So, feeling classical and pinky, I’ve decided to read <i>
  <b>Little Women</b>
</i>.<br>Finally.<br><br>I felt kind of intellectual when I carried it around with me in trains and subways, not gonna lie. But leaving that aside, I quite enjoyed it. I could actually label it as a cute and educative reading. But that's it.<br><br>There’s no much to say about it though. Everyone knows what’s it about. And if you don’t, you can find out what happens from <i>
  <b>Friends</b>
</i>. Rachel and Joey ruined this for me anyway, they could do that for you too.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1585145117ra/29167298.gif" width="278" height="278" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>I was under the impression that nothing really happened.</b><br><br>Even though there were a lot of little stories about our girls’ little “adventures”. Those adventures were more like cute day to day things than anything else and they did bring a smile to my lips from time to time.<br><br>The important thing about them though was that they taught you something of value, even though it was about pride, hope, health or social matters. You can count on those stories to help you be a tad better. At least I can honestly say that some of the advises in there gave me something to think about.<br><br>There is a sincere approach to matters. There is a society full of rules and labels. Meg, Jo, Beth and Amy must learn for themselves how to grow in order to make a good living in this society that is a bit cruel and unjust to women.<br><br>The girls have such different personalities and this is what makes them challenging. My personal favourite is Jo with whom I seem to resemble sometimes. She’s a wild spirit, free and in need of making herself heard. And also, her boyish air and the love for reading good books add to that aspect.<br><br>Beth is my second favourite because she’s sweet and innocent and knows how to love with depth.<br><br><br><i>
  <b>“There were many Beths in the world, shy and quiet, sitting in corners till needed, and living for others so cheerfully, that no one sees the sacrifices till the little cricket on the hearth stops chirping, and the sweet, sunshiny presence vanishes, leaving silence and shadow behind.”</b>
</i><br><br><br>Meg is the eldest so she has to take care of her sisters, to be their role model, but she loves luxury and wealth and is caught between two lives: the one she has and the one she wants to have.<br><br>Amy is a brat most of the time but sometimes she has her cute moments, I must say.<br><br>I’m totally in love with Jo and Laurie’s friendship though. There is something so warm and natural about it that it just fills my heart with joy and cuteness.<br><br><br><i>
  <b>“She could not speak, but she did “hold on”, and the warm grasp of the friendly human hand comforted her sore heart.”</b>
</i><br><br><br>Their love for each other, platonic love in its essence, is one of the best things this book has to offer. They’re both in for wide smiles and mischievous plans. And I love it.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1585145117ra/29167299.gif" width="305" height="236" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The empowering little speeches are a nice touch to the story and this is one bit of educative matter every single person who reads this book must extract and place somewhere near their heart. It is what makes this story one to never die.<br><br><br><i>
  <b>“Better be happy old maids than unhappy wives, or unmaidenly girls, running about to find husbands (...). Poverty seldom daunts a sincere lover.”</b>
</i><br><br><br>With that being said, what do you think about this piece of classic literature? Is it worth reading? Does it impress in any way?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1585145117i/29167300.png" width="291" height="409" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>(<a href="https://urcdn.eu/styles/eb32819f-7e53-4976-b950-2a6e8fa8a390_original?b" rel="nofollow noopener">Book-styled</a>)</span>

<div id="review_747911018" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/747911018" />

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/747911018?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stacey (prettybooks)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stacey (prettybooks)" href="/user/show/3533262-stacey-prettybooks">Stacey (prettybooks)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3533262-stacey-prettybooks?shelf=childrens-fiction">childrens-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3533262-stacey-prettybooks?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3533262-stacey-prettybooks?shelf=read-in-my-twenties">read-in-my-twenties</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3533262-stacey-prettybooks?shelf=classics-challenge">classics-challenge</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer747911018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10359154557958880368"><i>This mini review is part of a <a href='https://theprettybooks.wordpress.com/2015/01/17/mini-reviews-merry-christmas-and-other-stories-little-women-five-children-and-it/' rel='nofollow noopener'>blogpost talking about three children's classics</a>.</i><br/><br/><em>Little Women</em> was one of the classics that had been on my wishlist the longest. I<em> think</em> I first came across it while watching <em>that</em> episode of <em>Friends</em>. I didn't know much about Meg, Jo, Beth, and Amy March, but it seemed like the perfect children's classic for me.<br/><br/>Yet <em>Little Women</em> wasn't as engaging as I had hoped. I wasn't emotionally drawn into the sisters' lives, which is important for a character-driven novel. It's a</span>
  <span id="freeText10359154557958880368" style="display:none"><i>This mini review is part of a <a href="https://theprettybooks.wordpress.com/2015/01/17/mini-reviews-merry-christmas-and-other-stories-little-women-five-children-and-it/" rel="nofollow noopener">blogpost talking about three children's classics</a>.</i><br /><br /><em>Little Women</em> was one of the classics that had been on my wishlist the longest. I<em> think</em> I first came across it while watching <em>that</em> episode of <em>Friends</em>. I didn't know much about Meg, Jo, Beth, and Amy March, but it seemed like the perfect children's classic for me.<br /><br />Yet <em>Little Women</em> wasn't as engaging as I had hoped. I wasn't emotionally drawn into the sisters' lives, which is important for a character-driven novel. It's a great shame as I had high hopes. But I am struggling to decipher what exactly my issue <em>was</em>. I think I just wanted <em>more</em> to happen and more of an emotional punch – whether due to a sad story or a joyful one. It felt to me like the chapters could have been short stories rather than a linear storyline with a beginning, middle and end, even though it follows the lives of the sisters from childhood. I enjoyed some chapters quite a bit, whereas others not so much. And I was also surprised to discover that what I expected to happen in <em>Little Women</em> actually happens in the next book, <em>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78960.Good_Wives" title="Good Wives by Louisa May Alcott" rel="nofollow noopener">Good Wives</a>
</em>. I've not given up on <em>Little Women</em>, however. It's one I'll be coming back to, now that I know what to expect!<strong></strong></span>
  <a data-text-id="10359154557958880368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1873666500" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1873666500" />
    <a title="Brierly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25485458-brierly"><img alt="Brierly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421634217p2/25485458.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1873666500?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brierly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brierly" href="/user/show/25485458-brierly">Brierly</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25485458-brierly?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25485458-brierly?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1873666500" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8353343713566591001"><i>Little Women</i> ticks so many boxes for me -- 19th century lit, American lit, women's lit, and semi-autobiographical.<br/><br/>I do enjoy how Alcott repurposes people from her own family as the March sisters, but even more so I love the allegorical implications of the four young women. Each woman represents a different approach to life for 19th century American women. To marry and raise a family (Meg), to care for one's parents (Beth), to focus on art/pleasure (Amy), and to pursue a balanced life (Jo, based </span>
  <span id="freeText8353343713566591001" style="display:none"><i>Little Women</i> ticks so many boxes for me -- 19th century lit, American lit, women's lit, and semi-autobiographical.<br /><br />I do enjoy how Alcott repurposes people from her own family as the March sisters, but even more so I love the allegorical implications of the four young women. Each woman represents a different approach to life for 19th century American women. To marry and raise a family (Meg), to care for one's parents (Beth), to focus on art/pleasure (Amy), and to pursue a balanced life (Jo, based on Alcott herself). Meg/Beth are conforming characters, while Amy/Jo are certainly not (at first). As a whole, gender is fascinating in <i>Little Women</i>, especially in regards to Jo (masculine name, masculine traits, female) and Laurie (feminine name, feminine traits, male). Though, I was a bit surprised at <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[how quickly Jo transitions from precocious tomboy to a more traditional wife <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br /><i>Little Women</i> is largely an instructional guidebook for young women in America during this time period. There is quite a bit of emphasis on keeping busy with housework while Father is away fighting in the Civil War. I don't read this as particularly sexist, rather, invested in the Puritan work ideals of New England. I expected this book to be rather dry, but it has a lot of dimension. Definitely a few sections worth skimming, as this is long and somewhat episodic. This book is a historical document rather than a riveting read, but it highlights so many of my historical interests. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8353343713566591001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

They, in fact, pa</span>
  <span id="freeText7233086421609299049" style="display:none">I loved this book when I was a child. I kept reading it and wishing I was one of the famous March sisters. <br>I had a children edition and I don't know if there may be some strong differences from the adult one. I can say though that I would love reading it again in the non-children version.<br><br><i>Let's just admire the gorgeous cover of this beautiful edition.</i><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1447505845i/17014070._SY540_.jpg" width="350" height="490" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This book follows the life of <b>four</b> incredible <b>sisters</b>, Meg, Jo, Beth and Amy, detailing their passage from childhood to womanhood. They, in fact, pass through a series of events which leave a mark on each of them. Illness, war, marriage, love, death, work, responsabilities and, of course, sisterhood.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1447504387i/17013865.jpg" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Jo</b> has always been my favorite one of the group. I loved her strong character, the fact she adores literature, both reading and writing and that she isn't attracted by romance at all.<br><br><b>
  <i>“You are the gull, Jo, strong and wild, fond of the storm and the wind, flying far out to sea, and happy all alone.”</i>
</b><br><br>She is the main character and the one who leads the narration and after a crucial event in their life we can see how her narration signals a successfully completed adolescence. And that's the passage to the second part of their life.<br><br><b>
  <i>“I want to do something splendid...something heroic or wonderful that won't be forgotten after I'm dead. I don't know what, but I'm on the watch for it and mean to astonish you all someday.”</i>
</b><br><br>I was always fond of the atmosphere in this book. I could almost feel among the rest of them, it seemed I was there when they did something, I was feeling what they were feeling.<br>I appreciated the historic background and I loved it was set in the late 1800. I could imagine the clothes, the ball, the neighborhood, etc.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1447505168i/17014007._SY540_.png" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I have never seen the movie, but I would <b>love</b> to watch the 1994 edition, the one with Winona Ryder as Jo March. The cast looks wonderful and I literary can't wait to watch it.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1447505791i/17014066.jpg" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>
  <i>This book was for sure a childhood favorite.</i>
</b></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847495877" href="/book/show/30733607-little-women">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33212882-silke?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2875071261&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2875071261" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13289604003338078547">Well damn what can I say I LOVED THIS SO MUCH! This book holds a very special place in my heart. It’s so full of warm, fuzzy, happy moments and wonderful characters and I felt like my heart was about to explode the entire time I was reading. It really took me by surprise and I even dare say it’s become one of my favorites. I loved it so, so much!<br/><br/>So I didn’t know this but Little Women is sometimes published as two books in a series and with a slightly different storyline (in this edition Beth doe</span>
  <span id="freeText13289604003338078547" style="display:none">Well damn what can I say I LOVED THIS SO MUCH! This book holds a very special place in my heart. It’s so full of warm, fuzzy, happy moments and wonderful characters and I felt like my heart was about to explode the entire time I was reading. It really took me by surprise and I even dare say it’s become one of my favorites. I loved it so, so much!<br /><br />So I didn’t know this but Little Women is sometimes published as two books in a series and with a slightly different storyline (in this edition Beth doesn’t die, it’s all very confusing but also I kinda like it because honestly I was not ready for that to happen) which is the case with the edition I read. So even though I haven’t read the entire story yet and my experience deviates a bit from everyone else’s I still enjoyed this book immensely. I’m a little bit bummed about having picked up this edition because I feel like I’m missing out on stuff now. I’m definitely going to pick up the other edition where they’ve combined the two books in one because I want to experience this story to the fullest. I have to say I did really like the way this book ended. It actually has the ending that Alcott intended to write in the first place. It’s a very happy ending which is everything I want for these lovely characters. I like that I can leave it to my imagination to figure out what’s next for the March family. Also, the things I’ve heard about the sequel scare me (I’m in denial right now and want to believe that my ship doesn’t sink to the bottom of the ocean because WHYYYY) and I’m thinking that it might be a bit of a dissapointment if I do read it</span>
  <a data-text-id="13289604003338078547" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_443597527" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/443597527" />
    <a title="Girl with her Head in a Book" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book"><img alt="Girl with her Head in a Book" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351676097p2/14037242.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/443597527?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Girl with her Head in a Book" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Girl with her Head in a Book" href="/user/show/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book">Girl with her Head in a Book</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book?shelf=girls-are-awesome">girls-are-awesome</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book?shelf=children-s-book-corner">children-s-book-corner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14037242-girl-with-her-head-in-a-book?shelf=family-story">family-story</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer443597527" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13212097044377374655"> Oh, Little Women, how I love you.  My mother first read this to me when I was six.  Technically this is my second review of it as I also used it for my Book Report in Year 2.  I remember at the time that I kept being summoned out of class to show visitors to the school that I was reading Little Women - having worked as a primary school teacher, I now understand why the headmistress got so excited, it's a fair bit above the usual independent reading done by the average seven year-old.  In my Boo</span>
  <span id="freeText13212097044377374655" style="display:none"> Oh, Little Women, how I love you.  My mother first read this to me when I was six.  Technically this is my second review of it as I also used it for my Book Report in Year 2.  I remember at the time that I kept being summoned out of class to show visitors to the school that I was reading Little Women - having worked as a primary school teacher, I now understand why the headmistress got so excited, it's a fair bit above the usual independent reading done by the average seven year-old.  In my Book Report I advised people to read it because it had 'ups and downs just like real life' and that my favourite character was Beth.  It was around then that the 1994 film came out so a lot of my classmates were also aware of the story and in Year 4 some of them put on their own informal play of it which was highly interpretative - they assured the teacher that Pa March had died before the end and seemed strangely unappreciative when I pointed out indignantly that he hadn't.  Little Women has always been a Book that I Treasure.<br /><br />Still, after repeated readings, I actually hadn't looked at Little Women in about fifteen years.  I read How to be a Heroine recently though which had some rather disapproving insights into Alcott's most famous work.  According to Ellis, despite having loved it as a child, she found re-reading as an adult revealed Little Women as a mere conduct story, that the girls each had to be destroyed in their way in order to learn Valuable Lessons.  Thinking back, I could see what she meant - Jo's anger at Amy is punished by nearly losing her when Amy falls through the ice, Meg's vanity is rapped sharply at the ball, Amy's selfishness is caught by a humiliation at school and then Beth ... well.  Beth.  She is the Saintly Figure who is without sin and therefore naturally Doomed.  To be honest, I started re-reading it very much braced to hate it this time around.  But.  I didn't.<br /><br />The first chapter was not promising - we are introduced to each of the girls and they step on to the stage very much as cardboard cut-outs.  Meg is the lady-like one (being lady-like is a virtue which has really gone out of fashion in the twenty-first century), Jo is the tomboy, Beth is the sweet one and Amy is spoilt.  Each of the sisters speak in turn with Meg saying something lady-like, Jo speaking roughly about wanting to be a boy, Beth being sweet and Amy talking about Amy.  The 1994 adaptation takes a very feminist reading of Little Women and this in itself is an interpretative impression - Samantha Ellis is right that Louisa Alcott's book is at its heart a conduct story.<br /><br /><br />Lizzie Alcott<br />However.  It is important also to remember that a lot of this is based on Alcott's own experiences growing up as one of four sisters.  And this is most important of all when considering Beth March.  The Death of Beth is one of my early childhood traumas ... but it doesn't actually occur in Little Women contrary to popular belief but rather in the book's immediate sequel, Good Wives.  Still, it is signposted early on that she is one of those souls who nobody quite appreciates until they are gone - Alcott is talking about her own sister, Lizzie Alcott.  Lizzie.  Beth.  It's out in the open - Beth is Alcott's own adored sister who died young.  Then you realise that Beth is no mere stock Angel in the House character, she is a proxy for a flesh and blood young woman who was dearly loved and missed by her big sister who wrote a wonderful book that made millions of reader come to understand and share in her grief.  How many young girls have shed a tear over Beth's death?  It's actually a very beautiful thing to do - whether or not Lizzie really was as sweet-natured as Beth doesn't matter - she is being honoured by Louisa who really loved her and she has been brushed clean of any faults she might have had.<br /><br />A few years ago my mother's book group read Jane Eyre, one of my mother's very favourite books, and she was startled to encounter so much vitriol against Helen Burns, another saintly young Victorian girl who died young.  But again, Helen Burns is really just a proxy for Maria Bronte, adored elder sister to Charlotte who has also immortalised the first sister she lost by writing her for all the world to see.  It is a pure act of love.  In a more unsavoury manner, Dickens repeatedly used the memory of his unspoiled and virginal sister-in-law Mary Hogarth in various of his novels, most notably as Little Nell in The Old Curiousity Shop.  This was because he was grossed out by his own wife Catherine who had weight problems after giving birth to ten of his children but Mary died before she'd done anything yucky like have sex.  He used to publicly refer to the dead Mary as having been his ideal woman.  Dickens had issues.  He was a great writer but not so great with the women.<br /><br /><br />Beth is smallest, Amy = Elizabeth Taylor<br />Beth can be irritating and her adventures are less dramatic than those endured by the other March girls, as if Alcott is treating her gently.  The 1949 film (the Elizabeth Taylor one) casts Beth as the youngest March sister and it does make sense, she is the one who still likes dolls and rejects adulthood.  But in real life, Lizzie was the third Alcott sister and so she is in Little Women.  Beth's pure joy on receiving her piano and her thanks to Mr Laurence brought tears to my eyes - Alcott has made us all grieve for Lizzie and it feels churlish and unpleasant to have any other reaction to her character than sympathy.  Louisa Alcott, I am very sorry for your loss.  You clearly loved her very much.<br /><br />After the discussion in Chapter one about how they want to be good for their father while he is away being a chaplain in the Civil War, the girls wake up to find their mother has given them a book each for Christmas.  It is never quite clear whether this is a copy of Pilgrim's Progress or The Bible (or indeed a more metaphorical internal conscience?) but all the girls 'work' at their books throughout the year.  Jo tells her mother early on that she likes lessons that are not too 'preachy' and indeed Marmee March is very much the Voice Of God within the novel as she explains the moral of whatever has just occurred and then Jo provides a one-liner that drags the tone of the chapter back from direct sermonising.<br /><br /><br />(c) End of March<br />Jo's early lesson to control her temper is an interesting one to consider in our society today.  Amy's destruction of Jo's story is startlingly vicious particularly in contrast to the original offence and indeed Amy herself appears insensitive to the hurt she has caused.  In the twenty-first century we are told that we have 'freedom of speech', the internet allows people free rein to unleash their hatred.  Recently an internet troll who hounded the McCann family found herself unmasked and claimed that she was 'entitled' to exercise her views.  The internet has become a modern lynch mob - it turned on this particular woman and she herself committed suicide.  We are not taught to control our tempers any more.  We are not taught to consider whether our words are useful, kind or necessary.  I can see why a feminist reading might take this message to Jo as one intended to crush her independence but I don't actually think it is.  Amy is a little madam.  But Jo is the bigger person and she should not have let herself be dragged down to her level.<br /><br /><br />It's no secret that I espouse feminism but I think that they give this book an unfair rap.  Many of its values are old-fashioned but they do not necessarily deserve to be forgotten.  Disregarding vanity for domesticity is not that bad a lesson, for men or for women.  We should not devalue the home but rather celebrate the choice and those who choose it just as we celebrate those who do not.  It is the same as Susan Walker of Swallows and Amazons - she is not boring and I would want her around if I was going camping.  We can play around as much as we like and try to avoid responsibility but at the end of the day we will still be children, the adult thing is to look and see where our responsibilities lie.  It is a fairly stark contrast to modern teen literature where the ultimate goal is to Get The Guy but this is really about becoming a person you can respect.<br /><br />For the rest of my review - <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://girlwithherheadinabook.co.uk/2014/11/review-little-women-louisa-may-alcott.html">https://girlwithherheadinabook.co.uk/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13212097044377374655" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3227107718" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16938483782634464792">I... did not expect to love this?<br/><br/>This was written in the 19th century specifically for girls. We live in the 21st century. I'm a 21 year old guy and I enjoyed this so much.<br/><br/>Especially in times like these were everything seems to be spiralling out of our control, going absolutely bat shit crazy.<br/>If you are in need of an escape from all of this, Little Women is your answer!<br/><br/>Also I'm so mad that I only realised after finishing that this only part 1 so now I really need to get my hands on Little Wives!</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1116703494" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1116703494" />
    <a title="Kirstine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine"><img alt="Kirstine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505503603p2/5172557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1116703494?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirstine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirstine" href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine">Kirstine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781843915102" href="/book/show/21470612-little-women">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=1-women">1-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=r-2014">r-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1116703494" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8120965496428710213">The March family, with sisters Jo, Amy, Beth, Meg and their mother, Margaret, are a very charming acquaintance and following their big and small adventures in everyday life and love is a sweet and humorous ride. <br/><br/>Each chapter covers a new and different episode in their lives and there’s not any particular main plot. We simply get glimpses of their lives from one Christmas to the next. It makes it easy to dip in and out of, you don’t have to remember exactly what came before, but can read a chapte</span>
  <span id="freeText8120965496428710213" style="display:none">The March family, with sisters Jo, Amy, Beth, Meg and their mother, Margaret, are a very charming acquaintance and following their big and small adventures in everyday life and love is a sweet and humorous ride. <br /><br />Each chapter covers a new and different episode in their lives and there’s not any particular main plot. We simply get glimpses of their lives from one Christmas to the next. It makes it easy to dip in and out of, you don’t have to remember exactly what came before, but can read a chapter here and there, when you need a break from whatever dreary thing you’re doing. <br /><br />Most importantly, it’s fantastic to read a book where 5 women have center stage, while the men are reduced to minor, supporting characters. The sisters and their mother all vary in personalities and ages, and it’s wonderful to see them interact, clash, love and learn their life lessons as time goes by, always supporting each other. <br /><br />It’s a little heavy handed with the moralizing and slightly oversimplified, but it’s also meant for girls younger than I and a time much different from now. I wish I’d read it when I was a young girl myself, I think I would have liked it. <br /><br />It’s a nice little book to snuggle up with, when you need to feel a bit better about the world. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8120965496428710213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Library of a Moon Child" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59101965-library-of-a-moon-child"><img alt="Library of a Moon Child" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510918538p2/59101965.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3186435589?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Library of a Moon Child" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Library of a Moon Child" href="/user/show/59101965-library-of-a-moon-child">Library of a Moon Child</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3186435589" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6464164246913233395">Actual raring: 3.7⭐<br/><br/>Oh this was such a lovely story! I don't read many classics, but this one was something I quite enjoyed. We follow four sisters - Meg, Jo, Beth and Amy in their day-to-day lives during the American Civil war. I can't tell you much about the plot because there isn't really that much going on; the sisters try to work on their faults, meet new friends, have to overcome daily obstacles and deal with their poverty. They learn a lot from their experiences too and it's such a satisfa</span>
  <span id="freeText6464164246913233395" style="display:none">Actual raring: 3.7⭐<br /><br />Oh this was such a lovely story! I don't read many classics, but this one was something I quite enjoyed. We follow four sisters - Meg, Jo, Beth and Amy in their day-to-day lives during the American Civil war. I can't tell you much about the plot because there isn't really that much going on; the sisters try to work on their faults, meet new friends, have to overcome daily obstacles and deal with their poverty. They learn a lot from their experiences too and it's such a satisfaction watching them grow and mature. A the same time they're very entertaining and the story made me chuckle a lot. <br /><br />I was however surprised how forward-thinking the story was. From something written in the 1800, I expected very docile, "propper" ladies, but these girls were quite the feminists, especially Jo. They all worked and Jo defied the stereotiypical young lady in all ways imaginable - she wore what she wanted, ran instead of gracefully gliding when she felt like it, talked how she wanted and did all sorts of things that weren't fitting for a young lady back then. <br /><br />Even though I enjoyed their story, it lacked a proper plot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6464164246913233395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/593611087?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eldarwen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eldarwen" href="/user/show/19538634-eldarwen">Eldarwen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781407145433" href="/book/show/25890433-little-women">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19538634-eldarwen?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer593611087" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12409929915776910276">For some reason it took me ages to get to this book, even though I've heard so many good things about it - about the four lovely sisters, about how a lot of people read this book at least once every year and so forth.<br/><br/>I was not disappointed. Quite on the contrary. The book is well-written and the March sisters are a blast to read about. Their adventures, thoughts and their development over the course of just a single year were superb.<br/><br/>Reading this definitely made me realise that the moral values p</span>
  <span id="freeText12409929915776910276" style="display:none">For some reason it took me ages to get to this book, even though I've heard so many good things about it - about the four lovely sisters, about how a lot of people read this book at least once every year and so forth.<br /><br />I was not disappointed. Quite on the contrary. The book is well-written and the March sisters are a blast to read about. Their adventures, thoughts and their development over the course of just a single year were superb.<br /><br />Reading this definitely made me realise that the moral values put forth in the book are still very important to this day but seem to have started to be sadly forgotten.<br /><br />I can see myself rereading this at some point - following in the footsteps of so many others before me.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1704721394" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10672050456012619896">How dear Meg, Jo, Amy and Beth has become to me! Reading about all of their little troubles, adventures and thoughts have been most pleasant. Louisa May Alcott is indeed the American version of Jane Austen. Although the book rather more a selection of short stories than a complete saga, it was enjoyable to read. Dystopian stories may be in fashion today, but I prefer a story which makes me smile when reading. This one left me filled to the brim with joy! If you enjoyed the Little House at the Pr</span>
  <span id="freeText10672050456012619896" style="display:none">How dear Meg, Jo, Amy and Beth has become to me! Reading about all of their little troubles, adventures and thoughts have been most pleasant. Louisa May Alcott is indeed the American version of Jane Austen. Although the book rather more a selection of short stories than a complete saga, it was enjoyable to read. Dystopian stories may be in fashion today, but I prefer a story which makes me smile when reading. This one left me filled to the brim with joy! If you enjoyed the Little House at the Prairie, or any British classic, you'll like this book!</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6dOIjOg8jLz+CulSxwRR0ReN2s/zStQhF9Fkb96qzNGhAEFqZjOfMSTGI0x2wbCMKkdBw6fQNgchkaa0axSU+A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2SyajEOreqG9MbGCSR7lpQ6vWfbbu2QeIBL/B9/NPwmR/1NqzaRpLGf9e5z42wT4M2XC+o8hhjgWUj3canNnIA==')})
  }

To opowieść o sile, którą chwilami stać nawet na słabość. Autorka opowiada o kobietach jak nikt inny. „Hej, dziewczyno!” to świetnie skonstruowana, wciągająca opowieść o kobietach samodzielnych, błyskotliwych i odważnych. Feminizm, ale bez demagogii; teza – jednak bez publicystycznego koturnu, okraszona za to dowcipem, fantazją i komizmem sytuacyjnym. Zaborskie, te – jak pisze autorka – „wrzody na dupie” Krakowa, są wieczne. I właśnie to jest wspaniałe. Łukasz Maciejewski – krytyk filmowy i teatralny, wykładowca Szkoły Filmowej w Łodzi, członek Europejskiej Akademii Filmowej\n\nPisarstwo Patrycji Mnich przyciąga uwagę tworzeniem ludzkich portretów pisanych z czułością, ale bez idealizowania, ze zrozumieniem tego co w człowieku małe i duże. Umiejętność zauważenia i rozpoznania szczegółu, pojedynczego zdarzenia, zwrócenie uwagi na sprawy nieistotne, ukryte, poczucie humoru, dystans – to kolejne cechy wyróżniające tę prozę. Najnowsza książka w sposób nierozpoznany w polskiej literaturze jest próbą opisu pierwiastka żeńskiego z pełnym zrozumieniem dla jego wzlotu i upadku, dla jego niezwykłości i pospolitości, umiejętności przekraczania i banału. Ten ton jest nowy, odkrywczy i zasługuje na najwyższą uwagę. Tomasz Cyz – reżyser, eseista, były redaktor naczelny “Ruchu Muzycznego”, współtwórca i były naczelny “Dwutygodnika”\n\nPatrycja Mnich (dawniej Nowak) – jako autorka scenariuszy od kilkunastu lat stale współpracuje z telewizją TVN. Finalistka Konkursu Scenariuszowego Script Pro 2018 (dawniej Hartley-Merrill). Współautorka scenariusza do filmu fabularnego „Nad życie“, o historii siatkarki Agaty Mróz-Olszewskiej. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9177981868668553338">As <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2765771._A_M__Barnard' title=' A.M. Barnard' rel='nofollow noopener'> A.M. Barnard</a>: <br/>Behind a Mask, or a Woman's Power (1866)<br/>The Abbot's Ghost, or Maurice Treherne's Temptation (1867)<br/>A Long Fatal Love Chase (1866 – first published 1995)<br/>First published anonymously:<br/>A Modern Mephistopheles (1877)<br/><br/>Louisa May Alcott was born in Germantown, Pennsylvania on November 29, 1832. She and her three sisters, Anna, Elizabeth and May were educated by their father, philosopher/ t</span>
  <span id="freeText9177981868668553338" style="display:none">As <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2765771._A_M__Barnard" title=" A.M. Barnard" rel="nofollow noopener"> A.M. Barnard</a>: <br />Behind a Mask, or a Woman's Power (1866)<br />The Abbot's Ghost, or Maurice Treherne's Temptation (1867)<br />A Long Fatal Love Chase (1866 – first published 1995)<br />First published anonymously:<br />A Modern Mephistopheles (1877)<br /><br />Louisa May Alcott was born in Germantown, Pennsylvania on November 29, 1832. She and her three sisters, Anna, Elizabeth and May were educated by their father, philosopher/ teacher, Bronson Alcott and raised on the practical Christianity of their mother, Abigail May.<br /><br />Louisa spent her childhood in Boston and in Concord, Massachusetts, where her days were enlightened by visits to Ralph Waldo Emerson’s library, excursions into nature with Henry David Thoreau and theatricals in the barn at Hillside (now Hawthorne’s "Wayside").<br /><br />Like her character, Jo March in Little Women, young Louisa was a tomboy: "No boy could be my friend till I had beaten him in a race," she claimed, " and no girl if she refused to climb trees, leap fences...."<br /><br />For Louisa, writing was an early passion. She had a rich imagination and often her stories became melodramas that she and her sisters would act out for friends. Louisa preferred to play the "lurid" parts in these plays, "the villains, ghosts, bandits, and disdainful queens."<br /><br />At age 15, troubled by the poverty that plagued her family, she vowed: "I will do something by and by. Don’t care what, teach, sew, act, write, anything to help the family; and I’ll be rich and famous and happy before I die, see if I won’t!"<br /><br />Confronting a society that offered little opportunity to women seeking employment, Louisa determined "...I will make a battering-ram of my head and make my way through this rough and tumble world." Whether as a teacher, seamstress, governess, or household servant, for many years Louisa did any work she could find.<br /><br />Louisa’s career as an author began with poetry and short stories that appeared in popular magazines. In 1854, when she was 22, her first book Flower Fables was published. A milestone along her literary path was Hospital Sketches (1863) based on the letters she had written home from her post as a nurse in Washington, DC as a nurse during the Civil War.<br /><br />When Louisa was 35 years old, her publisher Thomas Niles in Boston asked her to write "a book for girls." Little Women was written at Orchard House from May to July 1868. The novel is based on Louisa and her sisters’ coming of age and is set in Civil War New England. Jo March was the first American juvenile heroine to act from her own individuality; a living, breathing person rather than the idealized stereotype then prevalent in children’s fiction.<br /><br />In all, Louisa published over 30 books and collections of stories. She died on March 6, 1888, only two days after her father, and is buried in Sleepy Hollow Cemetery in Concord.</span>

Here are talented tomboy and author-to-be Jo, tragically frail Beth, beautiful Meg<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7193058374456287811\" style=\"display:none\">Generations of readers young and old, male and female, have fallen in love with the March sisters of Louisa May Alcott’s most popular and enduring novel, Little Women. Here are talented tomboy and author-to-be Jo, tragically frail Beth, beautiful Meg, and romantic, spoiled Amy, united in their devotion to each other and their struggles to survive in New England during the Civil War.\nIt is no secret that Alcott based Little Women on her own early life. While her father, the freethinking reformer and abolitionist Bronson Alcott, hobnobbed with such eminent male authors as Emerson, Thoreau, and Hawthorne, Louisa supported herself and her sisters with &quot;woman’s work,” including sewing, doing laundry, and acting as a domestic servant. But she soon discovered she could make more money writing. Little Women brought her lasting fame and fortune, and far from being the &quot;girl’s book” her publisher requested, it explores such timeless themes as love and death, war and peace, the conflict between personal ambition and family responsibilities, and the clash of cultures between Europe and America.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7193058374456287811\" href=\"#\"
